This is information conceptually \\\"passed down\\\"\\n53:  * into parsing sub-nodes. This is like \\\"are we parsing strict code?\\\", and so\\n54:  * the parser can make decisions of how to parse based off that.\\n55:  *\\n56:  * Gathered-while-parsing information. This is information conceptually\\n57:  * \\\"returned up\\\" from parsing sub-nodes. This is like \\\"did we see a use strict\\n58:  * directive\\\"?\\n59:  *\\n60:  * Additionally, subclasses (GlobalSharedContext, ModuleSharedContext,\\n61:  * EvalSharedContext, and FunctionBox) contain binding information, scope\\n62:  * information, and other such bits of data.\\n63:  *\\n64:  * ParseContext.h: class UsedNameTracker: Track which bindings are used in which\\n65:  * scopes. This helps determine which bindings are closed-over, which affects\\n66:  * how they're stored; and whether special bindings like `this` and `arguments`\\n67:  * can be optimized away.\\n68:  *\\n69:  * ParseContext.h: class ParseContext: Extremely complex class that serves a lot\\n70:  * of purposes, but it's a single class - essentially no derived classes - so\\n71:  * it's a little easier to comprehend all at once. (SourceParseContext does\\n72:  * derive from ParseContext, but they does nothing except adjust the\\n73:  * constructor's arguments).\\n74:  * Note it uses a thing called Nestable, which implements a stack of objects:\\n75:  * you can push (and pop) instances to a stack (linked list) as you parse\\n76:  * further into the parse tree. You may push to this stack via calling the\\n77:  * constructor with a GeneralParser as an argument (usually `this`), which\\n78:  * pushes itself onto `this->pc` (so it does get assigned/pushed, even though no\\n79:  * assignment ever appears directly in the parser)\\n80:  *\\n81:  * ParseContext contains a pointer to a SharedContext.\\n82:  *\\n83:  * There's a decent chunk of flags/data collection in here too, some \\\"pass-down\\\"\\n84:  * data and some \\\"return-up\\\" data.\\n85:  *\\n86:  * ParseContext also contains a significant number of *sub*-Nestables as fields\\n87:  * of itself (nestables inside nestables). Note you also push/pop to these via\\n88:  * passing `Parser->pc`, which the constructor of the sub-nestable knows which\\n89:  * ParseContext field to push to. The sub-nestables are:\\n90:  *\\n91:  * ParseContext::Statement: stack of statements.\\n92:  * `if (x) { while (true) { try { ..stack of [if, while, try].. } ... } }`\\n93:  *\\n94:  * ParseContext::LabelStatement: interspersed in Statement stack, for labeled\\n95:  * statements, for e.g. `label: while (true) { break label; }`\\n96:  *\\n97:  * ParseContext::ClassStatement: interspersed in Statement stack, for classes\\n98:  * the parser is currently inside of.\\n99:  *\\n100:  * ParseContext::Scope: Set of variables in each scope (stack of sets):\\n101:  * `{ let a; let This is information conceptually \\\"passed down\\\"\\n53:  * into parsing sub-nodes. This is like \\\"are we parsing strict code?\\\", and so\\n54:  * the parser can make decisions of how to parse based off that.\\n55:  *\\n56:  * Gathered-while-parsing information. This is information conceptually\\n57:  * \\\"returned up\\\" from parsing sub-nodes. This is like \\\"did we see a use strict\\n58:  * directive\\\"?\\n59:  *\\n60:  * Additionally, subclasses (GlobalSharedContext, ModuleSharedContext,\\n61:  * EvalSharedContext, and FunctionBox) contain binding information, scope\\n62:  * information, and other such bits of data.\\n63:  *\\n64:  * ParseContext.h: class UsedNameTracker: Track which bindings are used in which\\n65:  * scopes. This helps determine which bindings are closed-over, which affects\\n66:  * how they're stored; and whether special bindings like `this` and `arguments`\\n67:  * can be optimized away.\\n68:  *\\n69:  * ParseContext.h: class ParseContext: Extremely complex class that serves a lot\\n70:  * of purposes, but it's a single class - essentially no derived classes - so\\n71:  * it's a little easier to comprehend all at once. interspersed in Statement stack, for classes\\n98:  * the parser is currently inside of.\\n99:  *\\n100:  * ParseContext::Scope: Set of variables in each scope (stack of sets):\\n101:  * `{ let a; let Certain limitations of\\n24:  * C++ (primarily the inability to partially specialize function templates),\\n25:  * plus the desire to minimize compiled code size in duplicate function\\n26:  * template instantiations wherever possible, mean that Parser exhibits much of\\n27:  * the same unholy template/inheritance complexity as token streams.\\n28:  *\\n29:  * == ParserSharedBase ==\\n30:  *\\n31:  * ParserSharedBase is the base class for both regular JS and BinAST parsing.\\n32:  * This class contains common fields and methods between both parsers. It stores\\n40:  * everything character- and handler-agnostic.\\n41:  *\\n42:  * ParserBase's most important field is the parser's token stream's\\n43:  * |TokenStreamAnyChars| component, for all tokenizing aspects that are\\n44:  * character-type-agnostic. {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01CWykPQx4DyJ5byNmYLJtdk","type":"tool_result","content":"{\"content\":\"1: /* -*- Mode: C; tab-width: 8; indent-tabs-mode: nil; c-basic-offset: 2 -*-\\n2:  * vim: set ts=8 sw=2 et tw=0 ft=c:\\n3:  *\\n4:  * This Source Code Form is subject to the terms of the Mozilla Public\\n5:  * License, v. 2.0. If a copy of the MPL was not distributed with this\\n6:  * file, You can obtain one at http://mozilla.org/MPL/2.0/. */\\n7: \\n8: #ifndef vm_Opcodes_h\\n9: #define vm_Opcodes_h\\n10: \\n11: #include <stddef.h>\\n12: #include <stdint.h>\\n13: \\n14: #include \\\"js/TypeDecls.h\\\"\\n15: \\n16: // clang-format off\\n17: /*\\n18:  * [SMDOC] Bytecode Definitions\\n19:  *\\n20:  * SpiderMonkey bytecode instructions.\\n21:  *\\n22:  * To use this header, define a macro of the form:\\n23:  *\\n24:  *     #define MACRO(op, op_snake, token, length, nuses, ndefs, format) ...\\n25:  *\\n26:  * Then `FOR_EACH_OPCODE(MACRO)` invokes `MACRO` for every opcode.\\n27:  *\\n28:  * Field        Description\\n29:  * -----        -----------\\n30:  * op           UpperCamelCase form of opcode id\\n31:  * op_snake     snake_case form of opcode id\\n32:  * token        Pretty-printer string, or null if ugly\\n33:  * length       Number of bytes including any immediate operands\\n34:  * nuses        Number of stack slots consumed by bytecode, -1 if variadic\\n35:  * ndefs        Number of stack slots produced by bytecode\\n36:  * format       JOF_ flags describing instruction operand layout, etc.\\n37:  *\\n38:  * For more about `format`, see the comments on the `JOF_` constants defined in\\n39:  * BytecodeUtil.h.\\n40:  *\\n41:  *\\n42:  * [SMDOC] Bytecode Invariants\\n43:  *\\n44:  * Creating scripts that do not follow the rules can lead to undefined\\n45:  * behavior. Bytecode has many consumers, not just the interpreter: JITs,\\n46:  * analyses, the debugger. That's why the rules below apply even to code that\\n47:  * can't be reached in ordinary execution (such as code after an infinite loop\\n48:  * or inside an `if (false)` block).\\n49:  *\\n50:  * The `code()` of a script must be a packed (not aligned) sequence of valid\\n51:  * instructions from start to end. Each instruction has a single byte opcode\\n52:  * followed by a number of operand bytes based on the opcode.\\n53:  *\\n54:  * ## Jump instructions\\n55:  *\\n56:  * Operands named `offset`, `forwardOffset`, or `defaultOffset` are jump\\n57:  * offsets, the distance in bytes from the start of the current instruction to\\n58:  * the start of another instruction in the same script. Operands named\\n59:  * `forwardOffset` or `defaultOffset` must be positive.\\n60:  *\\n61:  * Forward jumps must jump to a `JSOp::JumpTarget` instruction.  Backward jumps,\\n62:  * indicated by negative offsets, must jump to a `JSOp::LoopHead` instruction.\\n63:  * Jump offsets can't be zero.\\n64:  *\\n65:  * Needless to say, scripts must not contain overlapping instruction sequences\\n66:  * (in the sense of <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Overlapping_gene>).\\n67:  *\\n68:  * A script's `trynotes` and `scopeNotes` impose further constraints. Each try\\n69:  * note and each scope note marks a region of the bytecode where some invariant\\n70:  * holds, or some cleanup behavior is needed--that there's a for-in iterator in\\n71:  * a particular stack slot, for instance, which must be closed on error. All\\n72:  * paths into the span must establish that invariant. In practice, this means\\n73:  * other code never jumps into the span: the only way in is to execute the\\n74:  * bytecode instruction that sets up the invariant (in our example,\\n75:  * `JSOp::Iter`).\\n76:  *\\n77:  * If a script's `trynotes` (see \\\"Try Notes\\\" in JSScript.h) contain a\\n78:  * `JSTRY_CATCH` or `JSTRY_FINALLY` span, there must be a `JSOp::Try`\\n79:  * instruction immediately before the span and a `JSOp::JumpTarget immediately\\n80:  * after it. Instructions must not jump to this `JSOp::JumpTarget`. (The VM puts\\n81:  * us there on exception.) Furthermore, the instruction sequence immediately\\n82:  * following a `JSTRY_CATCH` span must read `JumpTarget; Exception` or, in\\n83:  * non-function scripts, `JumpTarget; Undefined; SetRval; Exception`. (These\\n84:  * instructions run with an exception pending; other instructions aren't\\n85:  * designed to handle that.)\\n86:  *\\n87:  * Unreachable instructions are allowed, but they have to follow all the rules.\\n88:  *\\n89:  * Control must not reach the end of a script. // [SMDOC] Script Stencil (Frontend Representation)\\n74: //\\n75: // Stencils are the set of data structures capturing the result of parsing and\\n76: // bytecode emission. The Stencil format is a precursor format that is then used\\n77: // to allocate the corresponding scripts on the GC heap that will be used for\\n78: // execution. By decoupling from the GC and other runtime systems, robust\\n79: // caching and speculation systems can be built that are more thread-agnostic\\n80: // and flexible.\\n81: //\\n82: // See: https://bugzil.la/stencil\\n83: //\\n84: // There are numerous data structures that make up the Stencil format. The\\n85: // structures are designed for fast serialization to and from disk by preferring\\n86: // indices over pointers and vectors instead of graphs to allow bulk operations.\\n87: //\\n88: //\\n89: // ParserAtom\\n90: // ----------\\n91: // Our parser relies on atomized strings as part of its normal operations and so\\n92: // a `ParserAtom` type exists that mirrors the `JSAtom` type but does not\\n93: // involve the garbage collector. This is possible because the lifetime of these\\n94: // ParserAtoms is the lifetime of the Stencil that makes use of them and we do\\n95: // not need finer grained collection.\\n96: //\\n97: //\\n98: // ScriptStencil\\n99: // -------------\\n100: // The key structures generated by parsing are instances of `ScriptStencil`.\\n101: // There is a `ScriptStencil` for the top-level script and for each inner\\n102: // function. It contains information needed to create the `JSFunction` (if it is\\n103: // a function) and the `BaseScript` (if not asm.js) and may or may not have\\n104: // bytecode. Each `ScriptStencil` may also reference the following Stencil types\\n105: // (similar to the `BaseScript::gcthings()` list):\\n106: //\\n107: //   * ParserAtom\\n108: //   * ScopeStencil\\n109: //   * RegExpStencil\\n110: //   * BigIntStencil\\n111: //   * ObjLiteralStencil\\n112: //   * StencilModuleMetadata\\n113: //\\n114: //\\n115: // CompilationStencil / ExtensibleCompilationStencil\\n116: // -------------------------------------------------\\n117: // Parsing a single JavaScript file may generate a tree of `ScriptStencil` that\\n118: // we then package up into the `ExtensibleCompilationStencil` type or\\n119: // `CompilationStencil`. They contain a series of vectors/spans segregated by\\n120: // data type for fast processing (a.k.a Data Oriented Design).\\n121: //\\n122: // `ExtensibleCompilationStencil` is mutable type used during parsing, and\\n123: // can be allocated either on stack or heap.\\n124: // `ExtensibleCompilationStencil` holds vectors of stencils.\\n125: //\\n126: // `CompilationStencil` is immutable type used for caching the compilation\\n127: // result, and is allocated on heap with refcount.\\n128: // `CompilationStencil` holds spans of stencils, and it can point either\\n129: // owned data or borrowed data.\\n130: // The borrowed data can be from other `ExtensibleCompilationStencil` or\\n131: // from serialized stencil (XDR) on memory or mmap.\\n132: //\\n133: // Delazifying a function will generate its bytecode but some fields remain\\n134: // unchanged from the initial lazy parse.\\n135: // When we delazify a function that was lazily parsed, we generate a new\\n136: // Stencil at the point too. ParserScopeSlotInfo<ModuleScope>;\n67: using ParserVarScopeSlotInfo = ParserScopeSlotInfo<VarScope>;\n68: \n69: using ParserBindingIter = AbstractBindingIter<TaggedParserAtomIndex>;\n70: using ParserPositionalFormalParameterIter =\n71:     AbstractPositionalFormalParameterIter<TaggedParserAtomIndex>;\n72: \n73: // [SMDOC] Script Stencil (Frontend Representation)\n74: //\n75: // Stencils are the set of data structures capturing the result of parsing and\n76: // bytecode emission. The Stencil format is a precursor format that is then used\n77: // to allocate the corresponding scripts on the GC heap that will be used for\n78: // execution. By decoupling from the GC and other runtime systems, robust\n79: // caching and speculation systems can be built that are more thread-agnostic\n80: // and flexible.\n81: //\n82: // See: https://bugzil.la/stencil\n83: //\n84: // There are numerous data structures that make up the Stencil format. The\n85: // structures are designed for fast serialization to and from disk by preferring\n86: // indices over pointers and vectors instead of graphs to allow bulk operations.\n87: //\n88: //\n89: // ParserAtom\n90: // ----------\n91: // Our parser relies on atomized strings as part of its normal operations and so\n92: // a `ParserAtom` type exists that mirrors the `JSAtom` type but does not\n93: // involve the garbage collector. This is possible because the lifetime of these\n94: // ParserAtoms is the lifetime of the Stencil that makes use of them and we do\n95: // not need finer grained collection.\n96: //\n97: //\n98: // ScriptStencil\n99: // -------------\n100: // The key structures generated by parsing are instances of `ScriptStencil`.\n101: // There is a `ScriptStencil` for the top-level script and for each inner\n102: // function. It contains information needed to create the `JSFunction` (if it is\n103: // a function) and the `BaseScript` (if not asm.js) and may or may not have\n104: // bytecode. Each `ScriptStencil` may also reference the following Stencil types\n105: // (similar to the `BaseScript::gcthings()` list):\n106: //\n107: //   * ParserAtom\n108: //   * ScopeStencil\n109: //   * RegExpStencil\n110: //   * BigIntStencil\n111: //   * ObjLiteralStencil\n112: //   * StencilModuleMetadata\n113: //\n114: //\n115: // CompilationStencil / ExtensibleCompilationStencil\n116: // -------------------------------------------------\n117: // Parsing a single JavaScript file may generate a tree of `ScriptStencil` that\n118: // we then package up into the `ExtensibleCompilationStencil` type or\n119: // `CompilationStencil`. They contain a series of vectors/spans segregated by\n120: // data type for fast processing (a.k.a Data Oriented Design).\n121: //\n122: // `ExtensibleCompilationStencil` is mutable type used during parsing, and\n123: // can be allocated either on stack or heap.\n124: // `ExtensibleCompilationStencil` holds vectors of stencils.\n125: //\n126: // `CompilationStencil` is immutable type used for caching the compilation\n127: // result, and is allocated on heap with refcount.\n128: // `CompilationStencil` holds spans of stencils, and it can point either\n129: // owned data or borrowed data.\n130: // The borrowed data can be from other `ExtensibleCompilationStencil` or\n131: // from serialized stencil (XDR) on memory or mmap.\n132: //\n133: // Delazifying a function will generate its bytecode but some fields remain\n134: // unchanged from the initial lazy parse.\n135: // When we delazify a function that was lazily parsed, we generate a new\n136: // Stencil at the point too. These delazifications can be merged into the\n137: // Stencil of the initial parse by using `CompilationStencilMerger`.\n138: //\n139: // Conversion from ExtensibleCompilationStencil to CompilationStencil\n140: // ------------------------------------------------------------------\n141: // There are multiple ways to convert from `ExtensibleCompilationStencil` to\n142: // `CompilationStencil`:\n143: //\n144: // 1. Temporarily borrow `ExtensibleCompilationStencil` content and call\n145: // function that takes `CompilationStencil` reference, and keep using the\n146: // `ExtensibleCompilationStencil` after that:\n147: //\n148: //   ExtensibleCompilationStencil extensible = ...;\n149: //   {\n150: //     BorrowingCompilationStencil stencil(extensible);\n151: ","url":"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/firefox--871325b8@01dcdcebaca08ed5c32f8d50532752a063dc5ade/-/blob/js/src/frontend/Stencil.h%3FL1-150"}}}
